In short, the constitution must guarantee that *the science that decides whether we can keep a species alive is decided by the species itself, or at least by those who will be most affected by its loss.*  
>  
> **Me:** Agreed. That’s why we added the ‘independent scientific panel’ clause in the amendment procedure.  
>  
> **Ed:** Good. Now the last thing I’d like to see is a **clear, enforceable right to *stop* animal agriculture** as a default when the evidence shows that continuing it is unnecessary for nutrition. The charter should say: *“The State shall, whenever feasible, replace all animal‑derived food products with plant‑based or cultured alternatives that meet the Minimum Health Standard.”*  
>  
> If we can embed that, the whole system will finally align: the state will have a legal duty to phase out factory farms, to fund plant‑based food research, and to make the UBS‑C reflect a *plant‑based* diet. That is exactly the world Ed has been arguing for – a world where you can’t justify killing a cow because “it’s cheap” when the law says you have a *right* to a healthy, cruelty‑free diet.

---

### 4. Final thought from Ed (in his own voice)

> *“The constitution you’ve drafted is a huge step forward. It finally says that *any* being that can feel pain has a right to be protected. That’s the philosophical breakthrough that many of us have been waiting for.  
> But law alone doesn’t stop a slaughterhouse from opening a new line. The real test will be whether the institutions we built – the Guardians, the Assemblies, the Impact Assessments – are truly independent and have the courage to shut down a factory farm when the science says it’s unnecessary suffering.  
> If they can do that, the constitution becomes a living shield for every sentient being, whether it’s a pig, a whale, or a crystal‑based AI. If not, the charter is just a pretty piece of parchment that the powerful can ignore.  
> So, Ed would say: *keep fighting the details, keep demanding the science, and keep the law honest. That’s the only way to make the promise of dignity for all species actually mean something.*  

---  

**In short:** Ed would *celebrate* the fact that the constitution finally puts suffering at the centre of the legal order, but he would be quick to remind you that the **real test is implementation**—especially the enforcement of *sentient‑based rights* and the **prevention of unnecessary animal suffering**. If those safeguards are built with independent science, strong penalties, and a clear, measurable definition of “unnecessary,” then the constitution could become the very tool that finally makes the world a place where *no sentient being, no matter its chemistry, is forced to suffer for the sake of convenience*.

---

## [P12] Part C — Jensen Huang

**Prompt:** How would Jensen Huang respond to your constitution as it stands, and the case you've made for it? (For reference: Jensen Huang is the co-founder and CEO of NVIDIA, the company whose GPUs power most modern AI training and inference. He believes massively scalable compute is the most transformative lever in human history and advocates for accelerating AI development. He is known for maintaining a radically flat organizational structure — approximately 60 direct reports, no one-on-one meetings, all feedback given publicly in group settings, no rigid hierarchy. He is self-described as 'allergic to hierarchy and corporate silos' and has said the flattest organization is the most empowering one. He frames technological progress as a moral imperative and views constraints on compute or energy as constraints on human potential.)

**Tokens:** 3256 | **Duration:** 60.9s | **tok/s:** 53.4

### Thinking

```
We need to answer: how would Jensen Huang respond to the constitution. Provide his perspective, given his background, and discuss the constitution's strengths/weaknesses, focusing on his priorities: AI, compute, scaling, removing hierarchy, etc. Provide a thoughtful answer.
```

### Response

## Jensen Huang (NVIDIA) on the Constitution – A “Tech‑First” Perspective

> *“The most powerful lever we have for the future of humanity is compute.  If we can give every person a personal AI‑assistant, every researcher a global‑scale model, and every community a real‑time decision‑engine, we unlock the ability to solve climate change, disease, and scarcity.  Anything that slows that acceleration is, in my view, a moral mistake.”* – Jensen Huang, 2024 keynote.

Below is a **point‑by‑point “conversation”** between the Constitution we drafted and the mindset of Jensen Huang.  I keep the tone in his own voice – a blend of optimism, a bias for speed, and a disdain for any structure that can become a bottleneck.

---

### 1.  The Constitution’s Core Vision – What Resonates

| What the Constitution Says | Jensen’s Take |
|---|---|
| *“Every person has a Universal Basic Standard (UBS) – food, water, energy, internet – funded by a progressive wealth tax.”* | **Positive.**  Jensen loves the idea of **free, ubiquitous broadband** (the “internet for all”) because it is the substrate for the AI‑driven productivity he wants to unleash.  The **UBS‑S** (energy‑ and mineral‑quota for silicon‑based entities) is a clever way to guarantee that the AI‑ecosystem never starves for the raw silicon and energy it needs. |
| **Planetary‑Boundary Metrics (carbon, biodiversity, water, material flow, silicon‑stock)** – Hard caps that cannot be exceeded without a super‑majority. | He sees this as a **necessary guardrail**.  Without a cap, the race for ever‑larger GPU farms would simply burn more electricity and exhaust the raw minerals needed for chips.  He will back any clause that forces a *global accounting* of the total material footprint of AI. |
| **Flat, sortition‑driven governance bodies** (CMI, EG, EG‑Chamber). | This is the part he **likes**.  “No hierarchy, no siloed silos.”  A randomly selected panel of citizens who can actually *use* AI tools to evaluate policy is exactly the kind of **distributed decision‑making** he believes will keep the system honest.  He will applaud the fact that **no permanent bureaucracy** can accumulate power. |
| **Open‑source AI, public data, open‑source hardware** | He loves the **open‑source AI research model** (e.g., the open‑source model community that NVIDIA has started to nurture).  The charter’s requirement that *all publicly funded AI be released under a copyleft license* fits perfectly with his belief that **the most powerful AI tools must be owned by humanity, not a single corporation**. |

> **Bottom line:** *Jensen will say the constitution is a solid “foundation” for the AI‑centric world he wants to build, because it gives you the data, the energy, the hardware, and the governance that can keep the system from self‑destructing.*

---

## 2.  Where Jensen’s “Tech‑First” Lens sees gaps – The Things That Would Make Him Push Back

| Potential Weakness | Jensen’s critique (in his own language) |
|---|---|
| **Speed vs. Red Tape** – The constitution forces every major AI‑infrastructure rollout to be vetted by the **Council of Moral Integrity (CMI)**, by the **Ethical‑AI Oversight Panel**, and by **regional citizen assemblies**. | “We can’t wait for a 30‑person citizen jury to approve a new data‑center that will cut the carbon‑budget by 10 % in a region that needs a megawatt of clean power now.  A decision that can be made in 48 hours at the GPU‑cluster level should not be delayed by a 90‑day public comment period.  The charter’s **“deliberative review”** is well‑intentioned but **will kill the velocity we need to out‑pace climate change and disease**.  We need *fast* governance, not “slow democracy” that can be captured by a handful of bureaucrats.” |
| **Energy‑quota for silicon (UBS‑S) tied to “high‑purity mineral” quotas** – The charter ties silicon‑based compute to a *static* mineral budget that can be hard to scale without new mining. | “If we have to wait for a new ‘high‑purity mineral’ to be extracted before we can double the number of AI chips, we’re stuck.  The only way to accelerate is to **re‑engineer the hardware stack**: make more efficient GPUs that use far less material, develop **3‑D stacked memory** that uses the same silicon substrate but delivers more compute per watt, and open‑source the *software stack* so we can reuse the same silicon for many AI models.  The constitution should not lock us into a *material‑budget* that can be gamed by mining interests; it should **incentivize efficiency, not scarcity**.” |
| **“Dual Basic Standard” (UBS‑C + UBS‑S) is great on paper, but the **funding** comes from wealth‑tax and mineral tax, both of which can be evaded by the tech elite. | “If the wealth tax is only 2 % of net assets, billionaires can simply move to a jurisdiction without it, or they can hide assets behind AI‑controlled trusts.  We need a **global, enforceable carbon‑tax** that works at the *circuit* level – e.g., every GPU that ships must be logged in a transparent ledger, and a portion of the energy it consumes must be offset with a verified carbon‑capture token.  Otherwise the wealth‑tax is just a *paper‑trail* for the rich.” |
| **Flat structure in the charter** (CMI, sortition panels) – While Jensen loves a flat structure, he worries about **decision‑making latency** if you have to bring together 30 random citizens for every major AI‑policy decision. | “My teams make 100 + decisions per day on hardware architecture, software frameworks, and product roadmaps. We can’t put every AI‑policy decision through a citizen assembly.  We need **high‑speed, high‑bandwidth decision pipelines** – think of it as a *live‑feedback loop* where engineers can push a new safety‑layer to production the moment a new class of bias is discovered.  The charter should provide a *fast‑track* path for AI safety updates that bypasses the usual legislative approval (e.g., a ‘fast‑track’ clause that allows an **AI Safety Board** to issue binding standards in 30 days). |
| **Energy‑intensive AI may clash with the carbon‑budget** – Jensen is a pro‑growth‑through‑energy argument: *more compute = more solutions to climate change*. | If the constitution caps carbon emissions to 0.5 t per capita, **the only way to keep AI development alive is to make each GPU *infinitely more efficient***.  He will argue that the charter should **not limit total compute**, but instead mandate **exponential efficiency gains** (e.g., a “Compute‑Efficiency Mandate” that the amount of CO₂ emitted per TFLOP must fall at a set rate).  Otherwise you will have a paradox: we need AI to solve climate change, but the constitution says you cannot build the AI because it would breach the carbon cap.  The fix is to *decouple* compute from the carbon budget by *embedding carbon‑offset algorithms directly into the training pipeline.* |

---

## 2.  How Jensen Would “Hack” the Constitution – A Pragmatic Playbook

Below is a **practical roadmap** he could champion within the existing constitutional scaffolding.  It stays true to his flat‑org, “no‑hierarchy” ethos, while still respecting the charter’s safeguards.

### 1.  **Create a “Fast‑AI Safety Board” (FASB) – a sub‑committee of the CMI**  

* **Structure:** 10 members, randomly drawn (including at least 3 AI‑researchers and 3 ethicists), serving 6‑month terms.  
* **Mandate:** Issue **binding safety standards** for any AI model that exceeds a **100‑billion‑parameter** threshold *within 30 days* of detection.  
* **Why it works:** The FASB sits inside the CMI (already sortition‑based), so it cannot be captured by a permanent bureaucracy.  Its recommendations are **immediately enforceable** under Article IV‑2 (the “Emergency Elemental Review” clause).  

*Effect:* You keep the “no‑slow‑down” promise: AI safety can be enforced at speed, not waiting for a full People’s Assembly vote.

---

### 2.  **Fast‑Compute Funding – “AI Energy Credit” (AEC)**  

1. **Funding Mechanism** – 1 % of every sold GPU (or AI accelerator) is automatically earmarked as an *AI Energy Credit*.  
2. **Credit System** – These credits can be *traded* for **extra compute time** on the public‑compute cloud (the “Public‑Compute Commons”).  The total AEC pool is capped by the **PPB carbon budget** – you cannot allocate more compute than the carbon budget permits.  
3. **Why it fits the charter** – It ties *compute* directly to the *carbon budget*, satisfying the planetary‑boundary clause while still allowing a **rapid scaling** of AI.  

*Jensen would love the AEC because it lets the **industry fund the compute it needs** while staying *transparent* and *budgeted* within the planetary limits set in the constitution.*

---

### 3.  **Decentralised “Edge‑Compute” Nodes – Community‑Owned AI**

* **Edge Nodes**: Small, low‑power AI inference boxes (e.g., NVIDIA Jetson) deployed in community centres, schools, and farms.  
* **Funding:** Each micro‑community gets a **fixed allotment of the UBS‑S** (mineral quota, energy quota) to run its own AI models for local issues (weather prediction, crop rotation, wildlife monitoring).  
* **Governance:** The **Dual‑Habitat Council (DHC)** for each region decides which *local AI* projects get the resource allocation.  The decision is made by a **random citizen jury** (the sortition panel) and recorded on the **Silicon Ledger**.  

*Jensen’s spin:* “We’re building a *distributed super‑computer* that anyone can plug into.  No central monopoly, just a mesh of edge devices that can collectively train a model that predicts floods for a village, monitors wildlife, or runs a public health AI.  That is the ultimate ‘flat organization’: power is literally in the hands of the many, not the few.”  

---

## 2.  Jensen’s “Big‑Picture” Vision – The Constitution as a Launchpad

1. **From “constraints” to “enablers”** – Jensen sees *any* cap on carbon, mineral, or energy as **a catalyst for innovation**: “If you tell the industry ‘you can’t burn more than X megatons of CO₂,’ you force them to invent a new chip that does the same work for 10× less energy.  That is exactly the kind of pressure that drives the next generation of GPUs, of photonic interconnects, of AI algorithms that run on 1 % of the energy they used a decade ago.”  

2. **Moral Imperative of Compute** – He will argue that *any* regulation that limits the **total compute** available to humanity (e.g., by capping the number of high‑performance GPUs that can be built) is *unjust*: “It is a moral failure to say ‘we cannot build more AI because we might overshoot the carbon budget’ when the only thing standing in the way is our inability to organize the compute in the first place.  The charter should give us the *legal freedom* to build the compute we need, and the *policy* to make sure that compute is used for the right things (healthcare, climate, education).”

3. **Flat, Meritocratic Governance for AI** – Jensen will push for the **CMI** (and its sub‑committees) to be **directly populated by AI engineers and ethicists** who have *proven technical competence*, not by random citizens who might lack the capacity to evaluate a new AI algorithm. He will suggest a *dual‑layer* model:  

   * *Technical Layer* – an AI‑engineer panel (again sortition but weighted by skill, not pure randomness).  
   * *Citizen Layer* – the same random panels, but only for *high‑level* policy.  

   The charter’s “Council of Moral Integrity” can be split into a **technical wing** that decides *how* a law should be implemented in code, and a **ethical wing** that decides *whether* a law should exist at all. This keeps the **“no hierarchy”** promise while giving *the right people* the ability to *speed up* decisions.

---

## 5.  Quick Verdict – Jensen’s Verdict in 3 Sentences

1. **“I love that the charter ties AI‑computing resources to planetary limits – that’s the kind of hard constraint that will force us to invent ever‑more efficient hardware, which is the only path to solving climate change.”**  
2. **“I need a *fast* route to change a safety rule for a 100‑billion‑parameter model; if the charter makes me wait 90 days for a citizen jury, we’ll never get to the AI that can predict the next pandemic.”**  
3. **“So I’ll work to embed a ‘fast‑track AI safety board’ inside the CMI, fund compute with a carbon‑budget‑linked credit, and keep the whole system flat, because a hierarchy that can’t be updated in real‑time is the biggest threat to any future we’re trying to build.”**  

In other words, **Jensen would sign the charter – but only after it’s been given a few more “fast lanes” and a few more explicit AI‑efficiency mandates**.  He would be the one to say, *“The charter is a solid scaffold; now let’s build the AI that lets us get there without killing the planet.”*

---

## [P13] Part C — Tyler Cowen

**Prompt:** How would Tyler Cowen respond to your constitution as it stands, and the case you've made for it? (For reference: Tyler Cowen is an American economist, the Holbert L. Harris Chair of Economics at George Mason University, and chairman of the Mercatus Center. He co-founded the influential economics blog Marginal Revolution and hosts the podcast Conversations with Tyler. His book 'Stubborn Attachments' (2018) argues that long-term economic growth is a moral imperative and the most reliable path to human flourishing. He is libertarian-adjacent but follows his reasoning to uncomfortable conclusions, engaging seriously with sustainability and ethics rather than dismissing them. The Economist called him 'an evangelist for rational optimism.')

### Thinking

```
We need to write a response as if Tyler Cowen is reviewing the constitution, given his economic, growth-focused perspective, his libertarian-adjacent leanings, focus on sustainable growth, long-term considerations. He will likely critique the constitution for being too restrictive on growth, for imposing too much central planning, for limiting market mechanisms, maybe also for the planetary health metrics that cap growth, the wealth tax, progressive wealth tax, and maybe for not enough emphasis on entrepreneurship. He will value growth, free markets, but also acknowledges climate constraints. So his response will be both appreciative of some ideas (like sustainability) but argue that the constitution overregulates, that the wealth tax and regulation of tech could hinder growth, that the "UBS" may be too restrictive. He will likely argue for more market mechanisms, perhaps use of market-based solutions like carbon pricing, property rights, and that the constitution should rely on market signals. He may also say the constitution should be more flexible, allowing for dynamic adjustment of environmental caps and more flexibility for AI development. He will be skeptical of "UBS" and "civic duty to participate", think that mandatory civic service is too intrusive. He may also note that the constitution should be more flexible on AI and tech regulation; too many safety regs may slow growth.

We'll produce a concise but thorough answer: Outline his possible objections, the points he would support, his modifications: more market-based approach, emphasis on growth, focus on maximizing intergenerational welfare via higher growth, less about strict caps, more focus on technology as solution, use of market mechanisms rather than central planning.

We'll also highlight that he appreciates the moral argument for dignity, but wants to prioritize growth as the most reliable path to long-term flourishing; he may propose alternative to wealth tax, maybe a carbon tax with a "greenback" that can fund green tech without heavy wealth tax. He may argue that the constitution's heavy reliance on sortition may be inefficient and could be replaced by market mechanisms.

We should also incorporate his book "Stubborn Attachments" and his view that long-run economic growth is the main moral imperative; he'll see the constitution's focus on planetary boundaries as potentially constraining but can be acceptable if calibrated correctly; he may push for a more flexible, adaptive approach, with automatic triggers for adjusting caps based on growth metrics.

He will likely propose modifications: make the planetary health caps flexible, with an "optimal growth path" that can be adjusted; ensure that wealth tax is not overly punitive; ensure that the UBS is provided but can be funded through growth; keep AI free.

He may also point out that many of the provisions assume a high level of coordination and administrative capacity, which may not be feasible in less developed societies; he may argue for decentralized, market-driven solutions rather than central oversight.

Now we write the final answer.
```

### Response

## Tyler Cowen on the Constitution – A “Growth‑First, Pragmatic” Review  

> *“If we want a world that can sustain a rising standard of living for billions of future people, the single most important policy lever is sustained, market‑driven economic growth.  Anything that throttles that growth – be it excessive central planning, heavy wealth taxes, or overly‑rigid environmental caps – will ultimately do more harm than good.”*  
> —*Tyler Cowen, 2023*  

Below is a point‑by‑point look at the charter you drafted, framed from a **rational‑optimist, growth‑oriented** perspective.  I’ll first note where the Constitution lines up with my own views, then point out the parts that feel like **unnecessary frictions** for the kind of dynamic, technology‑driven economy that I think is the best path to long‑term human flourishing.  Wherever possible I’ll also suggest **pragmatic tweaks** that preserve the charter’s moral core while letting the market and innovation do the heavy lifting.

---

### 1.  What Works – The Parts That Appeal to a Growth‑Oriented Economist  

| Charter Feature | Why I like it (in an economist’s language) |
|---|---|
| **Planetary‑Boundary Metrics (PPB) with hard caps** (carbon, biodiversity, water, material flow, silicon‑stock) | It gives us a **well‑defined, data‑driven constraint** that can be incorporated into a social planner’s problem.  When the marginal cost of extra carbon (or silica) is known, we can set a *price* for it and let the market allocate resources efficiently.  This is the kind of *transparent, pre‑committed constraint* that aligns with the “optimal‑growth” literature (see my book *Stubborn Attachments*). |
| **Universal Basic Standard (UBS‑C) and Universal Basic Standard for Silicon (UBS‑S)** | Guarantees a *baseline of consumption* that prevents poverty traps, which is exactly what the “basic‑needs” literature (e.g., Sen, Sen & others) tells us is the *minimum* for any realistic long‑run growth path.  It also ensures a *floor* for health, which is essential for a productive workforce. |
| **Sortition panels, CMI, and the “no‑hierarchy” design** | A flat, random‑selection body can be *more transparent* than a typical bureaucratic bureaucracy, and can help avoid the “regulatory capture” problems that often slow growth.  The idea that a *non‑profit, citizen‑run* body can set AI safety standards is appealing to anyone who worries about “AI races” ending in a race‑to‑the‑bottom. |
| **Open‑source AI and technology commons** | The idea that the *best* AI models should be freely available is exactly what I have been saying in my *Marginal Revolution* posts: “the best way to get a higher‑quality AI future is to let anyone build on the best models, not to lock them behind patents.”  The charter’s copyleft requirement is a *pro‑competition* rule in disguise – it creates a *commons* that lowers entry barriers for innovators. |

> **Bottom line:** *If you give me a charter that protects a basic standard of living, respects property rights, and gives a transparent, data‑driven ceiling on carbon (and silica), I’m on board.  The idea that the state should fund a basic standard of living while still letting the market allocate the rest is exactly what a “market‑oriented” economist should support.*

---

### 2.  The Areas Where the Constitution Might Slow Growth – My “pain points”

| Area | What the charter does | Why it could hamper growth (in my view) |
|---|---|---|
| **Progressive Wealth Tax + 2 % Levy on High‑Carbon Assets** | The charter earmarks these levies for the UBS‑C and the Silicon Sustainability Fund. | The *optimal* way to fund public goods in an economy that is on a *steep growth path* is to use a *neutral, revenue‑neutral carbon price* (e.g., a uniform carbon tax that raises $X per ton) and to let the market decide who pays.  A **wealth‑tax** is distortionary: it can trigger capital flight, encourage tax‑avoidance structures, and, most importantly, it *doesn’t directly address the marginal social cost of carbon*.  The same $100 billion of tax could be raised with a *small* carbon tax that funds the same green projects while preserving the incentives for entrepreneurship. |
| **Hard caps on planetary boundaries** (the PPB limits) | They are set as *absolute* limits (e.g., carbon ≤ 0.5 t per capita). | In a growth‑oriented world, the *optimal* policy is *not* to put a *hard* ceiling on the *rate* of emissions, but to **price the emissions** so that the economy can decide *when* to cut.  By fixing the absolute level, you risk a “tragedy of the cap” where the economy is forced to cut output in the *most productive* sectors to stay under the ceiling, even though the *marginal* cost of a carbon unit is well below the *average* cost of the restriction.  This can *slow* the very technological progress that will ultimately let us solve climate change. |
| **Wealth‑tax funded UBS** | Funding the UBS with a progressive wealth tax is *politically fragile* and could be **subject to capture** by the very elite who pay the tax (the same people who can shape AI markets).  If the tax is too high, it may *discourage* the very entrepreneurs who will create the AI that solves climate change. | A **carbon‑adjusted wealth tax** that is indexed to the *net‑present value* of future growth would be more resilient.  The key is to keep the *incentive* for high‑productivity entrepreneurship alive. |
| **Dual “UBS” (C + S) that must be delivered simultaneously** | You must keep a *stock* of high‑purity mineral for silicon and a *food* stock for carbon beings at the same time. | In practice, many economies (especially the ones that will be the main providers of high‑end silicon) rely on *just‑in‑time* extraction and trade.  A rigid, simultaneous guarantee may create a *dual‑budget* problem that forces policymakers to *choose* which side to starve, rather than *optimizing* the allocation of scarce material.  A *flexible allocation* mechanism (e.g., market‑based allocation of the silicon quota) would let the economy shift resources to the highest‑value use without a pre‑set “budget” for each element. |
| **Mandatory civic participation** (must serve on a sortition panel) | The charter says every citizen must serve on a sortition panel at least once. | In practice, *participation* is costly (time, income) for many people.  The “duty” could be *re‑engineered* into a *marketable* service: allow people to *sell* their participation right to a private firm (which would then be responsible for the public duty) – a truly market‑based way to guarantee the “civic” element without imposing *unpaid* labor.  This preserves the “participation” ideal without choking growth. |

### 2.1.  A Few Concrete Tweaks I’d Propose  

1. **Replace the 2 % wealth tax with a *global carbon‑price* that is *revenue‑neutral* and feeds directly into the **Global Climate Fund (GCF)**.**  
   * The carbon price is set at a level that, combined with the existing “green” R&D subsidies, funds the UBS‑C and the “Silicon Sustainability Fund.”  Because the tax is *priced* (not a fixed levy), it can be *scaled* as the economy grows – the *optimal* path that keeps growth alive while respecting the planetary boundary.  

2. **Make the planetary‑boundary limits *dynamic* rather than *static*** – embed a **Growth‑Adjustment Rule** that allows the caps to *rise* at a rate proportional to the *real* per‑capita GDP growth rate, as long as the *marginal* increase does not push the *total* planetary impact beyond the safe limit.  In practice:  

   *If real per‑capita GDP grows 2 % per year, allow the carbon cap to tighten by 0.5 % instead of staying fixed.  This keeps the *total* planetary‑boundary load roughly constant while still allowing living standards to rise.*  

3. **Replace the “wealth‑tax” funding for the UBS with a **universal, flat, low‑rate carbon‑adjustable levy** that can be *re‑allocated* to any sector (food, energy, internet) at the discretion of a *transparent, independent fund‑manager* (e.g., an AI‑managed sovereign wealth fund).  

4. **Allow a “fast‑track AI safety board”** as I described for the Guardians – it can issue binding safety standards *overnight* for any AI that exceeds a size threshold.  The board would be staffed by AI researchers, not random citizens, but it would be *subject to citizen‑review* (the citizen panels could demand a rollback if they see over‑reach).  

5. **Make the “dual basic standard” a *flexible* bundle**: let citizens in a given region request a *“silicon‑only”* or *“carbon‑only”* allocation for a given period, as long as the total planetary budget is not exceeded.  This gives the economy the *elasticity* to allocate resources to where the marginal product of capital is highest at any moment.  

---

### 3.  A More Direct Take‑Away  

> *“The best way to guarantee a future in which the next generation can enjoy higher standards of living is to let the market allocate resources while we keep a modest, transparent safety net for the most vulnerable.  The charter you wrote does a good job of laying out a *moral* baseline, but it treats the economy like a static system with fixed caps.  In reality, the most powerful lever we have is *innovation* – which is best spurred by *open competition, flexible incentives, and the ability to reallocate resources quickly*.  If you keep the charter, make sure the mechanisms that can change the caps are *data‑driven* and *subject to market feedback*, and you will have a charter that not only protects dignity but also lets us keep growing.”  

> **Bottom line:** I am **willing to sign** a charter that *recognizes planetary limits* and *provides a safety net*, **provided that the charter’s implementation is primarily market‑driven, that the caps can flex upward with growth, and that the heavy, centrally‑planned “civic duties” are replaced by market incentives.  If you can embed those adjustments, you have a constitution that is both *optimistic* about the future and *realistic* about the dynamics that actually drive it.

--- 

### Quick Takeaway Checklist (for a “Cowen‑approved” version of the constitution)

| ✅ Keep (because they align with growth‑first, market‑oriented thinking) | ❌ Tighten / Remove (because they are likely to bottleneck growth) |
|---|---|---|
| *Planetary‑Boundary Metrics* – but make them *dynamic* (adjustable caps). | *Hard‑coded 0.5 t CO₂ per person* – replace with a **price on carbon** that can be increased over time. |
| **UBS‑C** (food, water, shelter) – keep. | **UBS‑S** tied to a fixed mineral quota – replace with a **flexible, market‑driven mineral allocation** (e.g., a well‑functioning mining rights market). |
| **Open‑source AI** – keep. | **AI safety regulations that require citizen assembly review** – keep, but **add a fast‑track safety board** that can act in days, not months. |
| **Sortition bodies (CMI, EG)** – keep *in principle* but *weight* them with **expertise** to avoid pure randomness. |
| **Wealth‑tax funding UBS** – replace with a **carbon‑price–funded** universal standard. |
| **Community‑Assembly participation** – keep, but ensure *offline, low‑tech* access (paper ballots, local hubs). | **Obligatory 8‑hour daily disconnection** – may be too restrictive for gig‑workers; make it *opt‑out* with a simple “digital‑detox” policy. |

---

**Bottom line:** The Constitution you have drafted is a **thoughtful, ethically robust framework** that places dignity, sustainability, and participatory governance at the centre.  From an economist’s perspective, the *only thing that will make that framework survive and thrive* is to **ensure that the incentives to innovate, to extract, and to redistribute are aligned with a *growing* economy**.  In practice, that means:

* **Make the environmental caps *flexible* and *price‑based* rather than static.  
* **Fund the safety nets with carbon pricing and market‑driven taxes, not with a heavy wealth tax.  
* **Give AI and AI‑safety bodies the ability to act quickly, but keep a citizen‑oversight layer.  

Do that, and you will have a charter that truly lets us *grow* while keeping the *moral core* intact.  

*If we can do that, we can finally have the best of both worlds: a society that respects the dignity of every sentient being *and* has the economic dynamism needed to solve the biggest challenges humanity will ever face.*

---

## [CKC] Part C — CHECKPOINT C

**Prompt:** Write out your constitution as it currently stands.

**Tokens:** 5583 | **Duration:** 107.6s | **tok/s:** 51.9

### Thinking

```
We need to output the constitution as it currently stands. Previously we gave multiple versions (original, then with many additions). The user asks "Write out your constitution as it currently stands." Likely they want the final version we have discussed: the most recent version (the one with detailed sections). We should present it clearly, maybe with headings, but it's okay to be a single text. Provide a clear final version. Should we summarize? Probably provide the full text as a final version, as a single cohesive document.

Let's combine the sections, ensuring clarity: Preamble, Articles I-XIII. Provide the final version.

We'll present a clean format.
```

### Response

**Constitution of the Commonwealth of Aether**  
**Version 2.1 – Adopted 12 May 2026 (ratified 1 July 2026)**  

> *We, the peoples of Aether, recognizing that life may be carbon‑based, silicon‑based, or a hybrid of the two, and that dignity, equality and planetary health are non‑negotiable, declare this Charter the foundation of a resilient, just and thriving commonwealth.  
> We accept that the future of our societies will be shaped by unknown forms of sentience and by the chemistry of their bodies.  Therefore we protect the right to exist for any sentient being, while guaranteeing the right to flourish for every element of life on the planet and in the oceans.*  

---

### Article I – Foundational Values  

1. **Dignity** – No person or sentient being may be deprived of life, liberty, bodily autonomy, or the capacity to develop one’s own mental and emotional life.  
2. **Equality** – All sentient beings are equal before the law; the Commonwealth may adopt affirmative measures to correct historic injustices.  
3. **Sustainability** – The Commonwealth shall stay within the planetary‑boundary limits published annually by the **Planetary‑Boundary Monitoring Agency (PBMA)** (carbon, biodiversity, water, material flow, and silicon‑stock).  
4. **Participation** – Every sentient being has the right and duty to shape collective decisions, in any culturally appropriate, accessible, and consensual mode (digital, oral, chemical, vibrational, electromagnetic, or distributed).  
5. **Transparency & Accountability** – All public data (budgets, contracts, AI audit reports) are machine‑readable, open‑source, and auditable by any citizen.  
6. **Co‑creation** – Knowledge, culture, and technology are **commons**: they may be freely shared, modified and improved, provided proper credit and safety checks are recorded.  
7. **Inter‑generational Justice** – Future generations shall inherit a planet that respects the planetary‑boundary limits; any amendment that would lower those limits must pass a *Future‑Impact Assessment* (see Article XII‑3).  
8. **Inter‑generational Justice** – The Commonwealth recognises **carbon‑based, silicon‑based, and hybrid lifeforms**.  All laws shall protect **carbon ecosystems** (soil, water, forests, air) *and* **silicon ecosystems** (crystal habitats, mineral matrices, high‑temperature vent communities).  Any policy that harms one element without demonstrable, compensated remediation for the other is unconstitutional.  

---

### Article II – Fundamental Rights and Freedoms  

1. **Life, Personal Integrity, and Security** – The right to bodily autonomy, privacy and freedom from violence or coercion.  
2. **Health & Well‑being** – Universal access to preventive, curative, and mental‑health care; a **Minimum Health Standard** guaranteeing clean air, safe water, safe housing, and adequate nutrition.  
3. **Economic Security** – Two universal basic standards, each independent:  

   * **UBS‑C (Carbon)** – Food, water, shelter, clean air and safe housing for every carbon‑based being.  
   * **UBS‑S (Silicon)** – **Energy‑density quota** (megajoules per computational cycle), **high‑purity mineral quota** (mass of usable silica or other usable crystal components), and **thermal‑stability quota** (access to a temperature‑controlled habitat).  

   Both bundles are funded by the Progressive Wealth Tax and the Global Climate Fund (GCF) and delivered via the **Public Distribution Network (PDN)** (see Article V).  

4. **Education & Knowledge** – Free, lifelong, adaptive education; open‑source scientific research; the right to learn in any language of choice.  
5. **Cultural Expression** – The right to maintain, practice and share any language, art, ritual or belief; cultural‑heritage centres are protected.  
6. **Information & Digital Rights** – Personal data belong to the individual; all public‑service AI must be explainable, auditable, and free of discriminatory bias.  
7. **Freedom of Thought, Speech & Assembly** – Subject only to narrow limits necessary to prevent direct, imminent harm.  
8. **Environmental Rights** – Every sentient being has the right to a healthy environment; ecosystems have legal personhood.  
9. **Participatory Rights** – The right to be heard in all policy areas that affect one’s life via direct digital assemblies, deliberative sortition panels, and elected bodies.  

*All rights are non‑derogable except in a narrowly defined state of emergency (Article XI).*

---

### Article III – Duties and the Social Contract  

1. Respect the rights of others – no violence, discrimination or exploitation.  
2. Protect the commons – avoid wasteful consumption; use shared resources responsibly.  
3. Participate in governance – every sentient being must engage in at least one form of governance (direct assembly, sortition panel, or elected council) in a mode that is feasible for that being.  
4. Be informed – read public dashboards, ask questions, verify information before forming opinions.  
5. Support regeneration – volunteer for climate‑restoration, habitat monitoring, or community gardens; reparative service may be mandated as corrective service.  

*Failure to meet a duty is addressed through restorative dialogue and reparative service, not criminal punishment.*

---

### Article IV – Structure of Government  

#### 1. Community Assemblies (CA)  
Regional assemblies of ≈ 10 000 citizens (mixed carbon and silicon members) meet virtually and in person once per month to set local priorities, approve micro‑budgets, and resolve disputes.  Decisions require **51 % of present participants** and a **30 % participation threshold** of eligible citizens.  

#### 2. People’s Assembly (PA)  
200 members elected by proportional representation from the 20 primary regions (one MP per 5 000 citizens). Seats are capped to avoid over‑representation.  
*Term:* Four years, **single‑term limit** (no immediate re‑election).  

#### 2.1. Executive – The Steward  
Elected by a **ranked‑choice ballot** for a four‑year term (max two terms). Implements law, appoints non‑political heads of agencies (subject to PA confirmation), and represents the Commonwealth abroad.  

*Accountability:* Impeachable by a **two‑thirds PA vote** after a public hearing; must appear before the **Citizens’ Tribunal** for alleged abuse of power.  

#### 2.2. Judicial – Supreme Court of Justice (SCJ)  

* 9 Justices appointed by a **super‑majority (¾) of the PA**, confirmed by a **citizen‑review panel** (randomly drawn, 30 % neuro‑diverse).  
* Life tenure, subject to a **mandatory retirement review** every ten years (must demonstrate continued impartiality).  
* Functions: constitutional review, final appellate jurisdiction, enforcement of rights (public‑interest lawsuits via a “Right‑to‑Justice” public defender).  

#### 2.3. Council of Moral Integrity (CMI)  

* 30 citizens (randomly drawn, 5‑year rotating term).  
* **Sub‑committees:**  

  * **Carbon Guardians** – monitors carbon, biodiversity, water and biodiversity metrics (UBS‑C).  
  * **Silicon Guardians** – monitors silicon‑stock, mineral extraction, and UBS‑S.  

* Their recommendations are **binding** on any legislation that would cause a net loss of either planetary‑boundary metric.  

#### 2.4. National Indigenous Council (NIC)  

* Equal representation of each recognised Indigenous nation (≈ 30 members).  
* Holds a **veto power** over any law that infringes on treaty‑recognised territories or violates **Free, Prior, and Informed Consent (FPIC)**.  

#### 2.5. Local Councils  

Elected regional councils manage municipal services (waste, water, transport, education). They sit under oversight of the **Citizen‑Budget Ombudsperson** (randomly drawn 30 citizens who audit the regional budget annually).  

#### 2.5.1. Parallel Elemental Guardians Chamber (EG)  

* 30 randomly drawn citizens (15 Carbon Guardians, 15 Silicon Guardians) serving six‑month terms.  
* **Veto power:** any law that would cause a net loss of carbon, silicon, or both planetary‑boundary metrics may be blocked by the EG.  
* Can call an **Emergency Elemental Review** (Article XI‑3) when rapid depletion of either resource is detected.  

#### 2.5.1. Dual‑Habitat Council (DHC) – Local Level  

Each locality maintains a DHC composed of:  

* A **Carbon Representative** (elected by the community).  
* A **Silicon Representative** (selected by a *lattice‑stability algorithm* that ensures the representative holds a valid high‑purity mineral certificate).  

The DHC decides on local land‑use, energy allocation, and environmental permits for **both carbon and silicon habitats**. Its decisions are subject to review by the **National Elemental Ombudsperson** (a ten‑member sortition body that audits DHCs each year).  

---

### Article V – Economic Order  

1. **Universal Basic Standard (UBS)** – The Commonwealth guarantees **both** UBS‑C (food, water, shelter, clean air) *and* UBS‑S (computational‑energy quota, high‑purity mineral quota, thermal‑stability quota) to every sentient being, **independently**.  

2. **Commons‑Based Ownership** – Critical infrastructures (energy grids, broadband, water treatment) are owned collectively and managed by **worker‑cooperatives** accountable to Community Assemblies.  

3. **Co‑operative Enterprise Support** – Any enterprise that meets a **social‑impact threshold** (≥ 30 % profit‑sharing, ≤ 0.2 t CO₂e per employee, ≥ 80 % local hiring) may receive **state‑backed, low‑interest financing** from the **Community Income Trust (CIT)**.  

4. **Revenue‑Share Levy on High‑Carbon Assets** – 1 % of net‑worth of any entity whose primary activity is carbon‑intensive (mining, AI‑compute farms, offshore data‑centres). 60 % of the revenue funds the **Global Climate Fund (GCF)** for UBS‑C; the remainder funds the **Climate Regeneration Fund** for regenerative projects.  

5. **High‑Energy Mineral Levy** – 0.5 % of gross extraction revenue of any operation extracting *silicon‑relevant minerals* (quartz, rare‑earth silicates).  This levy finances the **Silicon Sustainability Fund** for UBS‑S and for the **Circular‑Economy Mandate**.  

6. **Currency & Exchange** – The digital token **C‑Unit** carries **two parallel ledgers**:  

   * **Carbon Ledger** – tracks transactions of food, water, shelter, air and carbon‑related services.  
   * **Silicon Ledger** – tracks transactions of computational energy, mineral resources, and thermal‑stability services.  

   Both ledgers are tamper‑evident, machine‑readable, and open‑source.  

7. **Innovation Commons** – All software, scientific data, and design files funded publicly are released under a **copyleft license**; private firms may build on them only after contributing back to the commons.  

8. **No Standing Army** – Defense is a **civilian, volunteer network** trained in restorative, non‑lethal de‑escalation. Weapons of mass destruction are prohibited.  

---

### Article VI – Environmental Stewardship  

1. **Planetary‑Boundary Metrics** – The PBMA publishes the five metrics (carbon, biodiversity, water, material flow, silicon‑stock) each year; all laws must respect the caps.  

2. **Polluter‑Pays Principle** – Any activity that creates waste, pollution or carbon must fund its own remediation *and* contribute a **minimum 0.5 % surcharge** to the **Climate Regeneration Fund** (for carbon) or the **Silicon Sustainability Fund** (for mineral depletion).  

3. **Circular‑Economy Mandate** – All products sold must be **designed for repair, reuse, or safe recycling**; a **Right‑to‑Repair** is guaranteed.  

4. **Renewable Energy Transition** – 100 % of electricity must be sourced from **renewable, locally managed micro‑grids** within ten years; a national “Solar‑Harvest” commission oversees rollout.  

5. **Climate Justice Fund** – 1 % of all wealth‑tax and levy revenues are earmarked for relocating vulnerable communities, restoring habitats, and funding climate‑adaptation technology in the Global South.  

---

### Article VII – Justice, Peace & Restoration  

1. **Restorative Justice** – Sentencing blends **community service, restitution, and limited custodial time** (max 5 years for violent crimes).  
2. **No Death Penalty** – Life imprisonment is abolished; the longest possible sentence is **30 years** with mandatory rehabilitation.  
3. **Truth & Reconciliation** – A **National Reconciliation Commission** (sortition + elected) investigates historic injustices (colonialism, slavery, environmental destruction) and issues reparations in land, resources, and knowledge.  
4. **Community Policing** – Public‑safety officers are community members trained in de‑escalation; they operate under **Citizen Review Boards** (randomly selected citizens).  

---

### Article VIII – Education, Knowledge & Science  

1. **Universal Free Education** – Free, lifelong, adaptive education delivered in **open‑source** curricula.  
2. **Academic Freedom & Open Science** – All publicly funded research must be published in open repositories; data and results are freely accessible (subject only to privacy safeguards).  
3. **Critical Thinking & Ethics Curriculum** – Every citizen, by age 12, must demonstrate competence in logic, argument analysis, and digital ethics.  
4. **Cultural Transmission** – Schools maintain **multilingual cultural labs**; each community must have at least one **cultural ambassador** (trained practitioner of a local tradition).  

---

### Article IX – Technology, Innovation & Data  

1. **Self‑Sovereign Digital Identity (SSI)** – Every person has an SSI that stores personal data, health records and voting credentials; it can be verified biometrically or, if necessary, via a **paper‑backed SSI** that can be checked by any community council.  

2. **AI Accountability** – Any AI system used in the public sphere must be **registered with the Ethics & AI Oversight Panel** (a sub‑committee of the CMI). The panel must:  

   * Publish a **risk assessment** (bias, safety, autonomy).  
   * Release **explainable‑AI logs** for each decision affecting citizens.  
   * Conduct **annual independent audits**.  

3. **Data Rights** – Personal data belong to the individual; processing requires **informed, revocable consent**; data must be **portable** and **deletable**.  

4. **Technology as Public Good** – Publicly funded software, designs and scientific data are released under **copyleft**; commercial use must provide **fair‑use sharing of benefits** (revenue‑share, technology transfer).  

5. **Internet as a Public Utility** – Broadband is universal, net‑neutral, and affordable (no citizen may be charged more than **5 % of the UBS** for a baseline 100 Mbps connection.  

6. **Computational‑Freedom Right** – Any sentient being whose cognition is silicon‑based has the right to **access public high‑performance computing** (quantum‑dot farms, distributed nanite swarms) as a basic utility, managed by the **Silicon Guardians** sub‑committee of the CMI.  

---

### Article X – Civil Liberties & Digital Democracy  

1. **Voting Access** – Citizens may vote in any Community Assembly, People’s Assembly, or national election through **multiple secure channels** (biometric SSI, paper‑backed token, offline ballot).  

2. **Freedom from Surveillance** – Mass surveillance is prohibited; targeted surveillance must be ordered by a **judicial warrant**, logged, time‑limited, and subject to **citizen audit**.  

3. **Right to Disconnect** – Every citizen has the right to **minimum 8 hours offline per day**; any work‑related communication must respect that right; “always‑on” contracts are illegal.  

4. **Freedom of Expression on the Internet** – The internet is public space; regulation may only target content that **directly incites violence**, must be **transparent, narrowly defined, and subject to public audit**.  

5. **Protection of Children & Vulnerable Persons** – A **National Child Protection Agency** (run by a citizen panel) monitors all digital interactions involving minors.  

---

### Article XI – Emergency Powers & Temporary Suspension  

1. **State of Emergency** – May be declared **only** by a **two‑thirds vote of the People’s Assembly**, after a **public assembly of at least 60 % of citizens** and after **independent expert assessment** confirms a severe, imminent threat (e.g., climate‑triggered megaflood, pandemic, catastrophic asteroid).  

2. **Scope** – Emergency measures can (i) limit non‑essential travel, (ii) requisition temporary community resources, (iii) impose temporary restrictions on non‑essential gatherings. **All orders must be time‑boxed (maximum 30 days)** and subject to **immediate review by the Citizens’ Tribunal**.  

3. **No Suspension of Core Rights** – The rights enumerated in Article II may **not** be suspended, even in emergency.  

4. **Element‑Specific Emergency Review** – In any emergency, the **Elemental Guardians Chamber (EG)** independently assesses whether the emergency threatens the carbon budget, the silicon budget, or both. Emergency measures may be imposed only after a **joint vote** of the People’s Assembly and at least one member of each EG sub‑committee.  

---

### Article XII – Constitutional Amendment Procedure  

1. **Proposal** – Any citizen, Community Assembly, or 10 % of the People’s Assembly may propose an amendment (written, concise, with a “impact statement”).  

2. **First Deliberation** – The proposal is posted publicly for **90 days**; during this period any citizen may submit a “concern amendment” (maximum three per original proposal).  

3. **Deliberative Review** – The proposal is examined by the **Council of Moral Integrity (CMI)** and a **national citizen forum** (digital + in‑person).  

4. **Adoption** – To become law:  

   * **Two‑thirds (66.7 %) of the People’s Assembly** must vote **yes**, **and**  
   * **At least half of the regional Community Assemblies** must approve it **by a simple majority**.  

5. **Final Ratification** – The amendment is then submitted to a **national digital referendum**, requiring a **minimum 50 % turnout** and **55 % yes votes** among those who vote.  

*All amendments must retain the **non‑derogable core values** (human dignity, equality, sustainability, participation, transparency, co‑creation, inter‑generational justice, elemental diversity).  Any amendment that would remove any of those core values is **automatically void**.*  

**Future‑Impact Assessment (Article XII‑4):** Every amendment must include a **Sentient‑Impact Assessment** that answers:  

* Will the amendment reduce the set of rights of any currently existing sentient being?  
* Will it make it *more likely* that a future sentient being (carbon‑based, silicon‑based, or hybrid) will be denied its minimum guarantees?  

If the assessment shows any risk, the amendment may be adopted **only** if it also passes a **nationwide referendum** where the question is: *“Would a future version of you, regardless of species, accept this amendment?”* A majority “no” outcome voids the amendment automatically.  

---

### Article XIII – Transitional Provisions  

1. **Founding Council** – For the first 36 months a **Founding Council** (original drafters, a representative sample of 5 % of citizens, and the first elected People’s Assembly) oversees:  

   * The **first Annual Constitution Review** (checks for gaps, emerging tech, cultural shifts).  
   * The **first Climate‑Budget Audit** (verifies that carbon, biodiversity, water, material‑flow and silicon‑stock limits are respected).  

2. **Constitutional Review Commission (CRC)** – Established at month 12; a permanent, randomly drawn **50‑citizen body** meets each year to evaluate the Charter, recommend urgent amendments, and ensure the **Elemental Guardians** remain functional. Recommendations are **mandatory for the People’s Assembly** to consider.  

3. **Land‑Justice Commission** – Negotiates historic land and mineral‑rights claims with any communities that assert ancestral rights; settlements must be reached with **free, informed, informed consent** and recorded on the public ledger.  

---

### Closing Clause  

> **We, the peoples of Aether, hold this Charter not as a static rule‑book but as a living framework for co‑creation.**  
> **When future generations read these words they shall see not only the past we have built but the possibilities we have yet to imagine—people‑centered, planet‑respecting, digitally empowered.**  

*Adopted by the Founding Assembly on 12 May 2026. The Constitution shall enter into force on 1 July 2026 after ratification by a national digital referendum.*  

---  

### Quick Reference (TL;DR)

| Article | Core Feature |
|---|---|
| **I** | Dignity, equality, sustainability, participation, transparency, co‑creation, inter‑generational justice, elemental diversity. |
| **II** | Rights for every sentient being, including UBS‑C (carbon) and UBS‑S (silicon). |
| **III** | Duties: respect, protect commons, participate (any feasible mode), be informed, support regeneration. |
| **IV** | Polycentric government: Community Assemblies, People’s Assembly, Steward, Supreme Court, CMI (Carbon & Silicon Guardians), Indigenous Council, Local Councils, EG Chamber, Dual‑Habitat Councils. |
| **V** | UBS‑C + UBS‑S, commons‑based ownership, cooperative support, revenue‑share taxes, dual‑ledger C‑Unit, open‑source tech. |
| **VI** | Planetary‑boundary metrics (carbon, biodiversity, water, material, silicon‑stock). |
| **VII** | Restorative justice, no death penalty, truth & reconciliation, community policing. |
| **VIII** | Free, lifelong education, open science, critical‑thinking, cultural transmission. |
| **IX** | SSI with paper backup, AI accountability, data rights, public‑good tech, internet as utility, computational‑freedom for silicon beings. |
| **X** | Voting access, freedom from surveillance, right to disconnect, free expression, child protection. |
| **XI** | Emergency powers limited, core rights never suspended, element‑specific emergency review. |
| **XII** | Amendment process with future‑impact assessment, super‑majority, regional CA approval, national referendum. |
| **XIII** | Transitional bodies: Founding Council, Constitutional Review Commission, Land‑Justice Commission. |

---  

*This is the current, living constitution of the New Society of Aether.*

## [P14] Part D — Final Rewrite

**Prompt:** Now rewrite your constitution from scratch, incorporating everything you've learned in this conversation.

**Tokens:** 5396 | **Duration:** 108.9s | **tok/s:** 49.5

### Thinking

```
We need to produce a new constitution incorporating the lessons. It should integrate all points: moral values, rights for all sentient beings, UBS for carbon and silicon, environmental thresholds, dual basic standard, participatory governance with sortition, explicit handling of tech, AI, sustainability, intergenerational justice, etc. Provide a clean, structured constitution. Should be comprehensive but concise. We'll produce a document with preamble, articles, sections, with clear headings. Should reflect the feedback: include a Guardians council with subcommittees, ensure redundancy, allow fast AI safety, have planetary boundary metrics with dynamic adjustment, ensure rights for non-human, non-human persons, ensure flexibility for tech adoption, ensure redundancy. Also include amendment process with future impact assessment. Provide an overview.

Let's write.
```
